<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Fascism" title="Portal:Fascism">Fascism portal</a><br/>
<span style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></span></td>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Neo-fascism" title="Template:Neo-fascism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Neo-fascism" title="Template talk:Neo-fascism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Neo-fascism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Authoritarianism</b> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> characterized by strong central power and limited <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">political freedoms</a>. Individual freedoms are subordinate to the state and there is no constitutional <a href="/wiki/Accountability" title="Accountability">accountability</a> under an authoritarian regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gap_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-gap-1">[1]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juan_Linz" title="Juan Linz">Juan Linz</a>'s influential 1964 description of authoritarianism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> characterized authoritarian <a href="/wiki/Political_system" title="Political system">political systems</a> by four qualities:</p>
<ol>
<li>limited <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(political_philosophy)" title="Pluralism (political philosophy)">political pluralism</a>; that is, such regimes place constraints on political institutions and groups like <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislatures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political parties</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interest_group" title="Interest group">interest groups</a>;</li>
<li>a basis for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_legitimacy" title="Political legitimacy">legitimacy</a> based on emotion, especially the identification of the regime as a necessary evil to combat "easily recognizable societal problems" such as <a href="/wiki/Underdevelopment" title="Underdevelopment">underdevelopment</a> or <a href="/wiki/Insurgency" title="Insurgency">insurgency</a>;</li>
<li>minimal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_mobilization" title="Social mobilization">social mobilization</a> most often caused by constraints on the public such as suppression of political opponents and anti-regime activity;</li>
<li>informally defined executive power with often vague and shifting powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>Modern <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">dictatorships</a> use an authoritarian concept to form a government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gap_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-gap-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritarian_government_and_states">Authoritarian government and states</span></h2>
<p>Linz distinguished new forms of authoritarianism from personalistic dictatorships and totalitarian states, taking <a href="/wiki/Francoist_Spain" title="Francoist Spain">Francoist Spain</a> as an example. Unlike personalistic dictatorships, new forms of authoritarianism have institutionalized representation of a variety of actors (in Spain's case, including the military, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Falange_Espa%C3%B1ola_y_de_las_JONS" title="Falange Española y de las JONS">Falange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monarchism" title="Monarchism">monarchists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technocrat" title="Technocrat">technocrats</a> and others); unlike totalitarian states, the regime relies on passive mass acceptance rather than popular support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several subtypes of authoritarian regimes have been identified by Linz and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> Linz identified the two most basic subtypes as <b>traditional authoritarian regimes</b> and <b>bureaucratic-military authoritarian regimes</b>:</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Traditional authoritarian regimes</b> are those "in which the ruling authority (generally a single person)" is maintained in power "through a combination of appeals to traditional legitimacy, <a href="/wiki/Clientelism" title="Clientelism">patron-client ties</a> and repression, which is carried out by an apparatus bound to the ruling authority through personal loyalties"; an example is <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haile_Selassie_I" title="Haile Selassie I">Haile Selassie I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Bureaucratic-military authoritarian regimes</b> are those "governed by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta">coalition of military officers</a> and technocrats who act pragmatically (rather than ideologically) within the limits of their bureaucratic mentality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mark_J._Gasiorowski" title="Mark J. Gasiorowski">Mark J. Gasiorowski</a> suggests that it is best to distinguish "simple military authoritarian regimes" from "bureaucratic authoritarian regimes" in which "a powerful group of technocrats uses the state apparatus to try to rationalize and develop the economy" such as <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> under <a href="/wiki/Park_Chung-hee" title="Park Chung-hee">Park Chung-hee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Linz also has identified three other subtypes of authoritarian regime: <b><a href="/wiki/Corporatism" title="Corporatism">corporatist</a></b> or <b>organic-statistic</b>, racial and <b>ethnic "democracy"</b> and <b>post-totalitarian</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Corporatist</b> authoritarian regimes "are those in which corporatism institutions are used extensively by the state to coopt and demobilize powerful interest groups"; this type has been studied most extensively in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Racial and ethnic "democracies"</b> are those in which "certain racial or ethnic groups enjoy full democratic rights while others are largely or entirely denied those rights," such as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Africa_under_apartheid" title="South Africa under apartheid">South Africa under apartheid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Post-totalitarian</b> authoritarian regimes are those in which totalitarian institutions (such as the party, <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">secret police</a> and state-controlled mass media) remain, but where "ideological orthodoxy has declined in favor of routinization, repression has declined, the state's top leadership is less personalized and more secure, and the level of mass mobilization has declined substantially."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_bloc" title="Eastern bloc">Eastern bloc</a> states in the mid-1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Authoritarian regimes are also sometimes subcategorized by whether they are <i>personalistic</i> or <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism"><i>populist</i></a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> Personalistic authoritarian regimes are characterized by arbitrary rule and <a href="/wiki/Authority" title="Authority">authority</a> exercised "mainly through patronage networks and coercion rather than through institutitions and formal rules."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> Personalistic authoritarian regimes have been seen in post-colonial Africa. By contrast, populist authoritarian regimes "are mobilizational regimes in which a strong, charismatic, manipulative leader rules through a coalition involving key lower-class groups."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> Examples include <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> under <a href="/wiki/Juan_Per%C3%B3n" title="Juan Perón">Perón</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-12"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> Egypt under Nasser,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gasiorowski_5-13"><a href="#cite_note-Gasiorowski-5">[5]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> under <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Chávez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro" title="Nicolás Maduro">Maduro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Authoritarianism is characterized by highly concentrated and <a href="/wiki/Centralized_government" title="Centralized government">centralized</a> power maintained by <a href="/wiki/Political_repression" title="Political repression">political repression</a> and the exclusion of potential challengers. It uses <a href="/wiki/Political_party" title="Political party">political parties</a> and mass organizations to mobilize people around the goals of the regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adam_Przeworski" title="Adam Przeworski">Adam Przeworski</a> has theorized that "authoritarian equilibrium rests mainly on lies, fear and economic prosperity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Authoritarianism also tends to embrace the informal and unregulated exercise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_(politics)" title="Power (politics)">political power</a>, a leadership that is "self-appointed and even if elected cannot be displaced by citizens' free choice among competitors," the arbitrary deprivation of <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>, and little tolerance for meaningful <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(politics)" title="Opposition (politics)">opposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>A range of <a href="/wiki/Social_control" title="Social control">social controls</a> also attempt to stifle <a href="/wiki/Civil_society" title="Civil society">civil society</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> while political stability is maintained by control over and support of the <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">armed forces</a>, a bureaucracy staffed by the regime, and creation of <a href="/wiki/Allegiance" title="Allegiance">allegiance</a> through various means of <a href="/wiki/Socialization" title="Socialization">socialization</a> and indoctrination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Authoritarian political systems may be weakened through "inadequate performance to demands of the people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup> Vestal writes that the tendency to respond to challenges to authoritarianism through tighter control instead of <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a> is a significant weakness, and that this overly rigid approach fails to "adapt to changes or to accommodate growing demands on the part of the populace or even groups within the system."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup> Because the <a href="/wiki/Legitimacy_(political)" title="Legitimacy (political)">legitimacy</a> of the state is dependent on performance, authoritarian states that fail to adapt may collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Authoritarianism is marked by "indefinite political tenure" of the ruler or <a href="/wiki/Ruling_party" title="Ruling party">ruling party</a> (often in a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a>) or other authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup> The transition from an authoritarian system to a more <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> form of government is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Democratization" title="Democratization">democratization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vestal_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-Vestal-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>John Duckitt suggests a link between authoritarianism and <a href="/wiki/Collectivism" title="Collectivism">collectivism</a>, asserting that both stand in opposition to <a href="/wiki/Individualism" title="Individualism">individualism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Duckitt writes that both authoritarianism and collectivism submerge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individual_rights" title="Individual rights">individual rights</a> and goals to group goals, expectations and <a href="/wiki/Conformity" title="Conformity">conformities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritarianism_and_totalitarianism">Authoritarianism and totalitarianism</span></h3>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Revolution_sidebar" title="Template:Revolution sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Revolution_sidebar" title="Template talk:Revolution sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Revolution_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">Totalitarianism</a> is an extreme version of authoritarianism. Authoritarianism primarily differs from totalitarianism in that social and economic institutions exist that are not under governmental control. Building on the work of Yale political scientist Juan Linz, Paul C. Sondrol of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Colorado_at_Colorado_Springs" title="University of Colorado at Colorado Springs">University of Colorado at Colorado Springs</a> has examined the characteristics of authoritarian and totalitarian dictators and organized them in a chart:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sondrol_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sondrol-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th></th>
<th>Totalitarianism</th>
<th>Authoritarianism</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Charisma" title="Charisma">Charisma</a></td>
<td>High</td>
<td>Low</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Role conception</td>
<td>Leader as function</td>
<td>Leader as individual</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Ends of power</td>
<td>Public</td>
<td>Private</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">Corruption</a></td>
<td>Low</td>
<td>High</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Official <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a></td>
<td>Yes</td>
<td>No</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Limited <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(political_philosophy)" title="Pluralism (political philosophy)">pluralism</a></td>
<td>No</td>
<td>Yes</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Legitimacy</td>
<td>Yes</td>
<td>No</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Sondrol argues that while both authoritarianism and totalitarianism are forms of <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocracy</a>, they differ in "key <a href="/wiki/Dichotomy" title="Dichotomy">dichotomies</a>":</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>(1) Unlike their bland and generally unpopular authoritarian brethren, totalitarian dictators develop a <a href="/wiki/Charisma" title="Charisma">charismatic</a> '<a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">mystique</a>' and a mass-based, pseudo-democratic <a href="/wiki/Interdependence" title="Interdependence">interdependence</a> with their followers via the conscious manipulation of a prophetic image.</p>
<p>(2) <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Concomitant" title="wikt:Concomitant">Concomitant</a> role conceptions differentiate totalitarians from authoritarians. Authoritarians view themselves as individual beings largely content to control, and often maintain, the status quo. Totalitarian self-conceptions are largely <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleological</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrant</a> is less a person than an indispensable 'function' to guide and reshape the universe.</p>
<p>(3) Consequently, the utilisation of power for personal aggrandizement is more evident among authoritarians than totalitarians. Lacking the binding appeal of <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a>, authoritarians support their rule by a mixture of instilling fear and granting rewards to loyal collaborators, engendering a <a href="/wiki/Kleptocracy" title="Kleptocracy">kleptocracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sondrol_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sondrol-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Compared to totalitarianism, "the authoritarian state still maintains a certain distinction between state and society. It is only concerned with political power and as long as that is not contested it gives society a certain degree of liberty. Totalitarianism, on the other hand, invades <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Private_life" title="Private life">private life</a> and asphyxiates it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinpoes_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cinpoes-14">[14]</a></sup> Another distinction is that "authoritarianism is not animated by utopian ideals in the way totalitarianism is. It does not attempt to change the world and human nature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinpoes_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cinpoes-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Joachim_Friedrich" title="Carl Joachim Friedrich">Carl Joachim Friedrich</a> writes that "a totalist ideology, a party reinforced by a <a href="/wiki/Secret_police" title="Secret police">secret police</a>, and monopoly control of ... industrial mass society" are the three features of totalitarian regimes that distinguish them from other autocracies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cinpoes_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cinpoes-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritarianism_and_democracy">Authoritarianism and democracy</span></h3>

<div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left">Principal concerns</div>
<div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Individualism_sidebar" title="Template:Individualism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Individualism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Individualism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Individualism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Authoritarianism and democracy are not fundamentally opposed to one another; it is thus definitely possible for democracies to possess strong authoritarian elements, for both feature a form of submission to authority. An <a href="/wiki/Illiberal_democracy" title="Illiberal democracy">illiberal democracy</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Procedural_democracy" title="Procedural democracy">procedural democracy</a>) is distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Substantive_democracy" title="Substantive democracy">substantive democracy</a>) in that illiberal democracies lack the more democratic features of liberal democracies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Judicial_independence" title="Judicial independence">independent judiciary</a>, along with a further distinction that liberal democracies have rarely made war with one another. More recent research has extended the theory and finds that more democratic countries tend to have few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Militarized_Interstate_Dispute" title="Militarized Interstate Dispute">Militarized Interstate Disputes</a> causing less battle deaths with one another, and that democracies have much fewer <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil wars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some commentators, such as <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Martin_Lipset" title="Seymour Martin Lipset">Seymour Martin Lipset</a>, believed that low-income authoritarian regimes have certain technocratic, "efficiency-enhancing advantages" over low-income democracies, helping authoritarian regimes generate development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halperin_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halperin-17">[17]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morton_H._Halperin" title="Morton H. Halperin">Morton H. Halperin</a>, Joseph T. Siegle, and Michael M. Weinstein (2005) counter this belief, arguing that the evidence has showed that there is no "authoritarian advantage" and that there is a "democratic advantage" instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halperin_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halperin-17">[17]</a></sup> Halperin et al. argue that democracies "realize superior development performance" over authoritarianism. They point out that poor democracies are more likely to have steadier economic growth, and less likely to experience economic and humanitarian catastrophes, than authoritarian regimes; that civil liberties act as a curb on corruption and misuse of resources; and that democracies are more adaptable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halperin_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Halperin-17">[17]</a></sup> Halperin point out that the vast majority of refugee crises and financial catastrophes occur in authoritarian regimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halperin_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Halperin-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies suggest that several health indicators (life expectancy and infant and maternal mortality) have a stronger and more significant association with democracy than they have with <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a> per capita, size of the public sector, or income inequality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Prominent economist <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> has theorized that no functioning liberal democracy has ever suffered a large-scale <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9,_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1386" data-file-width="1360" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg/170px-Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg/255px-Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg/340px-Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9%2C_2013.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Salman_bin_Abdull_aziz_December_9,_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Salman_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Salman of Saudi Arabia">Salman</a>, King of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Research shows that the democratic nations have much less <a href="/wiki/Democide" title="Democide">democide</a> or murder by government. However, it should be noted that those were also moderately developed nations before applying liberal democratic policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Research by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> suggests that political institutions are extremely important in determining the prevalence of <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a>, and that parliamentary systems, political stability and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_press" title="Freedom of the press">freedom of the press</a> are all associated with lower corruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> One study has concluded that terrorism is most common in nations with intermediate <a href="/wiki/Political_freedom" title="Political freedom">political freedom</a>. The nations with the least amount of <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorism</a> are the most and least democratic nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_states_considered_to_be_authoritarian">Examples of states considered to be authoritarian</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Mugabe_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="297" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Robert_Mugabe_cropped.jpg/170px-Robert_Mugabe_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Robert_Mugabe_cropped.jpg/255px-Robert_Mugabe_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2a/Robert_Mugabe_cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>

<a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a></div>

<p>There is no precise definition of authoritarianism, but several annual measurements are attempted, including <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a>’s annual <a href="/wiki/Freedom_in_the_World" title="Freedom in the World">Freedom in the World</a> report.</p>
<p>The following is a non-exhaustive list of <i>examples</i> of states which are currently (or frequently) characterized as authoritarian:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ilham_Aliyev" title="Ilham Aliyev">Ilham Aliyev</a> (2003-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Khalifa" title="House of Khalifa">House of Khalifa</a> (1746-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> under <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Lukashenko" title="Alexander Lukashenko">Alexander Lukashenko</a> (1994-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuters1_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuters1-26">[26]</a></sup> on account of Lukashenko's self-described <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> style of government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HRW_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-HRW-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Khmer_Rouge" title="Khmer Rouge">Khmer Rouge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hun_Sen" title="Hun Sen">Hun Sen</a> (1985-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> under <a href="/wiki/Paul_Biya" title="Paul Biya">Paul Biya</a> since 1982<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedom_House_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freedom_House-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Amnesty_International_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Amnesty_International-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">Communist Party of China</a> (1949-). “Some scholars have deemed the Chinese system a 'fragmented authoritarianism' (<a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Lieberthal" title="Kenneth Lieberthal">Lieberthal</a>), a 'negotiated state' or a 'consultative authoritarian regime.'”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> under <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%BAl_Castro" title="Raúl Castro">Raúl Castro</a> (1959-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> under <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Fattah_el-Sisi" title="Abdel Fattah el-Sisi">Abdel Fattah el-Sisi</a> (2014-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini" title="Ruhollah Khomeini">Ruhollah Khomeini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ali_Khamenei" title="Ali Khamenei">Ali Khamenei</a> (1981-).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Linz wrote in 2000 that “it is difficult to fit the Iranian regime into the existing typology, as it combines the ideological bent of totalitarianism with the limited pluralism of authoritarianism and holds regular elections in which candidates advocate differing policies and incumbents are often defeated.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> under <a href="/wiki/Nursultan_Nazarbayev" title="Nursultan Nazarbayev">Nursultan Nazarbayev</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedom_House_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freedom_House-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Lao_People%27s_Revolutionary_Party" title="Lao People's Revolutionary Party">Lao People's Revolutionary Party</a> (1975-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Kim_dynasty_(North_Korea)" title="Kim dynasty (North Korea)">Kim dynasty</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_Workers%27_Party" title="Korean Workers' Party">Korean Workers' Party</a> (1947-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> under <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> (1999-) (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Putinism" title="Putinism">Putinism</a> for more) – described as "really a mixture of authoritarianism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Managed_democracy" title="Managed democracy">managed democracy</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saud" title="House of Saud">House of Saud</a> (1744-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> under <a href="/wiki/Omar_al-Bashir" title="Omar al-Bashir">Omar al-Bashir</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedom_House_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Freedom_House-31">[31]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafez_Al-Assad" title="Hafez Al-Assad">Hafez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bashar_al-Assad" title="Bashar al-Assad">Bashar al-Assad</a> (1970-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> under General <a href="/wiki/Prayut_Chan-o-cha" title="Prayut Chan-o-cha">Prayut Chan-o-cha</a> who overthrew the democratically elected government of <a href="/wiki/Yingluck_Shinawatra" title="Yingluck Shinawatra">Yingluck Shinawatra</a> in a military coup and installed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta">military junta</a> to oversee the governance of Thailand (2014-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recep_Tayyip_Erdogan" title="Recep Tayyip Erdogan">Recep Tayyip Erdogan</a> (2003-) – described as a “competitive authoritarian regime”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> under Saparmurat Nyazow (1991-2006) and Gurbanguly Berdimuhamedow (2006-) <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> under Islam Karimov (1989 to 2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a> under <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Hugo Chávez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nicol%C3%A1s_Maduro" title="Nicolás Maduro">Nicolás Maduro</a> (1999-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vietnamese_Communist_Party" title="Vietnamese Communist Party">Vietnamese Communist Party</a> (1976-)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a> (1980-).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_states_which_were_historically_authoritarian">Examples of states which were historically authoritarian</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Engelbert_Dollfuss.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="697" data-file-width="572" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Engelbert_Dollfuss.png/170px-Engelbert_Dollfuss.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Engelbert_Dollfuss.png/255px-Engelbert_Dollfuss.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Engelbert_Dollfuss.png/340px-Engelbert_Dollfuss.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Engelbert_Dollfuss.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Engelbert_Dollfuss" title="Engelbert Dollfuss">Engelbert Dollfuss</a>'s chancellorship in Austria contained many authoritarian elements.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Franco0001.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="310" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Franco0001.PNG/125px-Franco0001.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Franco0001.PNG/188px-Franco0001.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Franco0001.PNG/250px-Franco0001.PNG 2x" width="125"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Franco0001.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caudillo" title="Caudillo">caudillo</a> of Spain from 1936 to 1975, led an authoritarian regime that persisted until his death and the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_transition_to_democracy" title="Spanish transition to democracy">Spanish transition to democracy</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Argentine_Revolution" title="Argentine Revolution">Argentine Revolution</a> period of military rule (1966–1973)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and later during the <a href="/wiki/Peronism" title="Peronism"><i>justicialista</i></a> rule of <a href="/wiki/Juan_Per%C3%B3n" title="Juan Perón">Juan Perón</a> (populist authoritarianism).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> during both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Brazil)" title="Estado Novo (Brazil)">Estado Novo</a> period under <a href="/wiki/Get%C3%BAlio_Vargas" title="Getúlio Vargas">Getúlio Vargas</a> (1937–1945) and under <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_military_government" title="Brazilian military government">military government</a> from a <a href="/wiki/1964_Brazilian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1964 Brazilian coup d'état">1964 coup</a> until a transition to democracy in the early and mid-1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a> from a <a href="/wiki/1962_Burmese_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1962 Burmese coup d'état">1962 coup</a> until a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011%E2%80%9312_Burmese_political_reforms" title="2011–12 Burmese political reforms">transition to democracy</a> beginning in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> under <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a> from 1973 until a <a href="/wiki/Chilean_transition_to_democracy" title="Chilean transition to democracy">transition to democracy</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> under <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hosni_Mubarak" title="Hosni Mubarak">Hosni Mubarak</a> from 1952 to 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> under <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> from 1969 until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_Civil_War" title="2011 Libyan Civil War">his deposition and death in 2011</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> under <a href="/wiki/Antanas_Smetona" title="Antanas Smetona">Antanas Smetona</a> from late 1926 until the <a href="/wiki/1940_Soviet_ultimatum_to_Lithuania" title="1940 Soviet ultimatum to Lithuania">Soviet ultimatum of 1940</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Macedonia" title="Republic of Macedonia">Republic of Macedonia</a> under <a href="/wiki/Nikola_Gruevski" title="Nikola Gruevski">Nikola Gruevski</a> (2006 to 2016)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Estado Novo regime</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">António de Oliveira Salazar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marcelo_Caetano" title="Marcelo Caetano">Marcelo Caetano</a> from 1932 to 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> under <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Francisco Franco</a> from 1936 to 1975, when the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_transition_to_democracy" title="Spanish transition to democracy">Spanish transition to democracy</a> began after Franco's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> under the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a> from 1948 until the end of <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> from 1948<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> until a transition to democracy in 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> from 1945 until the transition to democracy in 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> from 1925 through 1945, when a transition to democracy began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Authoritarian_weakness_and_resilience">Authoritarian weakness and resilience</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Andrew_J._Nathan" title="Andrew J. Nathan">Andrew J. Nathan</a> notes that "regime theory holds that authoritarian systems are inherently fragile because of weak legitimacy, overreliance on coercion, overcentralization of decision making, and the predominance of personal power over institutional norms....Few authoritarian regimes—be they communist, fascist, corporatist, or personalist—have managed to conduct orderly, peaceful, timely, and stable successions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NathanAuthoritarian_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-NathanAuthoritarian-72">[72]</a></sup> One exception to this general trend is the endurance of the authoritarian rule of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a>, which has been unusually resilient among authoritarian regimes. Nathan posits that this can be attributed to four factors: (1) "the increasingly norm-bound nature of its succession politics"; (2) "the increase in meritocratic as opposed to factional considerations in the promotion of political elites"; (3) "the differentiation and functional specialization of institutions within the regime"; and (4) "the establishment of institutions for political participation and appeal that strengthen the CCP's legitimacy among the public at large."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NathanAuthoritarian_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-NathanAuthoritarian-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-authoritarianism">Anti-authoritarianism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-authoritarianism" title="Anti-authoritarianism">Anti-authoritarianism</a></div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> there was a strong sense of anti-authoritarianism based on <a href="/wiki/Anti-fascism" title="Anti-fascism">anti-fascism</a> in Europe. This was attributed to the active resistance from <a href="/wiki/German-occupied_Europe" title="German-occupied Europe">occupation</a> and to fears arising from the development of <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpowers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swcjsb_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-swcjsb-73">[73]</a></sup> Anti-authoritarianism also became associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countercultural" title="Countercultural">countercultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bohemian" title="Bohemian">bohemian</a> movements such as the <a href="/wiki/Beat_Generation" title="Beat Generation">Beat Generation</a> in the 1950s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hippies" title="Hippies">hippies</a> in the 1960s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stonepolitics_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stonepolitics-75">[75]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Punk_subculture" title="Punk subculture">punks</a> in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_and_authoritarianism">Gender and authoritarianism</span></h2>
<p>According to a study by Brandt and Henry there is a direct correlation between the rates of <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">gender inequality</a> and the levels of authoritarian ideas in the male and female populations. It was found that in countries with less gender equality where individualism was encouraged and men occupied the dominant societal roles, women were more likely to support traits such as obedience which would allow them to survive in an authoritarian environment, and less likely to encourage ideas such as independence and imagination. In countries with higher levels of gender equality, men held less authoritarian views. It is theorized that this occurs due to the stigma attached to individuals who question the cultural norms set by the dominant individuals and establishments in an authoritarian society as a way to prevent the psychological stress caused by the active ostracizing of the stigmatized individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
